com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=576963765"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=576963765"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=576963765"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}},{"id":32518,"link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/samsung\/","name":"Samsung","slug":"samsung","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/32518"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=32518"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=32518"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=32518"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=32518"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}}],[],[],[],[]]}},{"id":2279125,"date":"2022-03-03T11:30:25","date_gmt":"2022-03-03T19:30:25","guid":{"rendered":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/?p=2279125"},"modified":"2022-03-02T14:56:33","modified_gmt":"2022-03-02T22:56:33","slug":"putting-the-autonomous-cart-before-the-robotic-horse","status":"publish","type":"post","link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/03\/putting-the-autonomous-cart-before-the-robotic-horse\/","title":{"rendered":"Putting the autonomous cart before the robotic horse"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\"><span class=\"featured__span-first-words\">I\u2019m writing this<\/span> fresh off hosting <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/02\/agility-robotics-and-playground-global-join-techcrunch-live-to-speak-on-fundraising-robotic-delivery\/\">TechCrunch Live this week<\/a> with Jonathan Hurst of Agility Robotics and Bruce Leak of Playground Global. I\u2019ll be posting more about the session later this week, but in the meantime, it\u2019s got me thinking about carts, horses and the inherent ordering system therein.<\/p>\n<p>Specifically, how important is it that a robotics startup have a specific notion about things like addressable market when starting out? It\u2019s a complex question with no single answer and one that\u2019s largely dependent on the startup and the investors. Agility showed off its Series A\/seed (it\u2019s complicated) pitch deck this week, which was more a highlight of the company\u2019s technology than a sophisticated breakdown of market fit.<\/p>\n<p>If your technology is compelling enough &#8212; and you find the right firm &#8212; you might be able to get away with this. Of course, in addition to some really cool YouTube videos (which did, indeed, play a role in getting on Playground\u2019s radar), the startup also proved that it was able to create, build and sell robots on a limited budget and headcount. All told, the company sold around a dozen Cassie robots, which largely went out to research facilities.<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2279125\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_1929362\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption alignnone\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-1929362\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout size-full wp-image-1929362\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2020\/01\/digit-1.jpg\" alt=\"agility robotics digit\" width=\"1024\" height=\"728\" srcset=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2020\/01\/digit-1.jpg 1280w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2020\/01\/digit-1.jpg?resize=150,107 150w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2020\/01\/digit-1.jpg?resize=300,213 300w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2020\/01\/digit-1.jpg?resize=768,546 768w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2020\/01\/digit-1.jpg?resize=680,483 680w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2020\/01\/digit-1.jpg?resize=50,36 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" \/><p id=\"caption-attachment-1929362\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:<\/strong> Ford\/Agility Robotics<\/p><\/div><\/p>\n<p>Nice work if you can get it. There\u2019s a reason some larger corporations continue to target research facilities, even if it\u2019s sometimes a bit of a loss leader. Imagine training a future generation of roboticists on your specific platform. Talk about ivy league grass roots growth. And hey, the ones that develop really clever applications could well find a way onto your payroll in the future.<\/p>\n<p>That said, the seeds of future growth were in the pitch deck as well. In addition to current applications like warehouse fulfillment, the pitch does highlight Digit\u2019s potential as a developer platform. This is clearly the direction things have been going for a while beyond the world of single-purpose robotics. Anyway, good talk. More information on that will be hitting TC in the next couple of days.<\/p>\n<p>This week\u2019s a bit of a slow one, in terms of big robotics headlines, though we\u2019ve got some funding rounds to discuss. First up is the world of last-mile delivery robotics, which apparently is an excuse to print money these days. I\u2019ve seen a lot of verticals make out like fundraising bandits during the pandemic, but I\u2019m having trouble remembering the last time a week went by without a big delivery round.<\/p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n<p>Lots of hungry VCs out there sitting around their deserted offices, wondering where their food is, I guess.<\/p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2261759\" style=\"width: 850px\" class=\"wp-caption alignnone\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2261759\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout size-full wp-image-2261759\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/Starship-Technologies-2.jpeg\" alt=\"\" width=\"840\" height=\"472\" srcset=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/Starship-Technologies-2.jpeg 840w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/Starship-Technologies-2.jpeg?resize=150,84 150w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/Starship-Technologies-2.jpeg?resize=300,169 300w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/Starship-Technologies-2.jpeg?resize=768,432 768w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/Starship-Technologies-2.jpeg?resize=680,382 680w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/01\/Starship-Technologies-2.jpeg?resize=50,28 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 840px) 100vw, 840px\" \/><p id=\"caption-attachment-2261759\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:<\/strong> Starship Technologies<\/p><\/div><\/p>\n<p>This week, <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/01\/starship-technologies-raises-another-42m-to-fuel-the-growth-of-its-fleet-of-self-driving-delivery-robots\/\">Starship Technologies returns to the well<\/a>. A $42 million Series B puts the company north of $100 billion all told. NordicNinja and Taavet+Sten led and TDK Ventures and Goodyear (yes, that TDK and that Goodyear) returned for the round. Fun nugget from Ingrid\u2019s write up that I\u2019m just going to go ahead and quote straight up for you:<\/p>\n<blockquote><p>\u201cThere is so much more to be done. We are still in grand scheme of things just getting started,\u201d Ahti Heinla, the CTO who co-founded Starship with Janus Friis (the Skype co-founder, where Heinla was a key early developer), said in an interview.<\/p>\n<p>On the form factor, he confirmed that one idea that the company is working on in its R&amp;D labs is a robot that will travel not just on the sidewalk, as the current model does, but also the road, which will also bring it into the realm of working on \u201cbigger robots.\u201d<\/p>\n<p>\u201cI wouldn\u2019t be surprised if we do something like that in the future, although the sidewalk strategy is still a good choice for the majority of deliveries out there.\u201d<\/p><\/blockquote>\n<p>For now, though, a small cart with a bunch of wheels is really the path of least resistance &#8212; unless you\u2019re Agility, I suppose.<\/p>\n<p>Drone delivery, on the other hand, feels so hit or miss these days. There are broader (and important) questions around how much we really want or need a sky full of drones. Amazon drones, Google drones, UPS and Fedex drones all whirring above our heads, getting our lattes to us while they\u2019re still reasonably warm. I\u2019ve long noted that they make sense for more rural areas (battery and range depending). Certainly getting someone in some remote and hard to reach location their medicine is the sort of application we can all rally behind.<\/p>\n<p>But Alphabet\u2019s Project Wing has long had urban regions on its list, including the Australian capital. This week, the company reached <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/01\/alphabets-wing-drones-hit-200000-delivers-as-it-announces-supermarket-partnership\/\">200,000 commercial deliveries.<\/a> That comes six months after the firm hit the 100,000 milestone, 30,000 of which happened in Australia in the first months of 2022.<\/p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2277784\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption alignnone\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2277784\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout size-full wp-image-2277784\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/wing.jpeg\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"683\" srcset=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/wing.jpeg 2048w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/wing.jpeg?resize=150,100 150w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/wing.jpeg?resize=300,200 300w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/wing.jpeg?resize=768,512 768w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/wing.jpeg?resize=680,453 680w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/wing.jpeg?resize=1536,1024 1536w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/wing.jpeg?resize=50,33 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" \/><p id=\"caption-attachment-2277784\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:<\/strong> Wing<\/p><\/div><\/p>\n<p>The news finds Wing partnering with major Australian supermarket chain Coles in Canberra. It will join KFC, Vietnamese restaurant chain Roll\u2019d and Friendly Grocer, which is providing rapid COVID tests through this service. Says Wing:<\/p>\n<blockquote><p>Integrating drone delivery into daily life isn\u2019t just an added convenience. It holds the promise to reduce traffic congestion, accidents, and greenhouse gas emissions while growing sales for businesses all the while giving people more time back in their busy lives. If you want a peek into that future, just look to Australia.<\/p><\/blockquote>\n<p>With both drones and infrastructure top of mind, <a href=\"https:\/\/www.cs.cmu.edu\/news\/2022\/monitoring-bridge-safety\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">CMU is showcasing<\/a> a crack-detection system for bridges and other infrastructure, created in a joint development with Tokyo-based construction firm Shimizu Corp. The pair have created a prototype drone designed to seek out problem areas before they, well, become much larger problems.<\/p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2279127\" style=\"width: 910px\" class=\"wp-caption alignnone\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2279127\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout size-full wp-image-2279127\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/drone-bridge-web.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"900\" height=\"506\" srcset=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/drone-bridge-web.png 900w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/drone-bridge-web.png?resize=150,84 150w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/drone-bridge-web.png?resize=300,169 300w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/drone-bridge-web.png?resize=768,432 768w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/drone-bridge-web.png?resize=680,382 680w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/drone-bridge-web.png?resize=50,28 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 900px) 100vw, 900px\" \/><p id=\"caption-attachment-2279127\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:<\/strong> CMU<\/p><\/div><\/p>\n<p>&#8220;The automated technology we developed for the Shimizu project is designed to prevent this type of collapse via comprehensive mapping, crack detection and structural analysis that would be too much work if it were done by hand,&#8221; says CMU associate research professor Sebastian Scherer. &#8220;Today, typically you only do spot checks on critical parts, since an exhaustive survey and analysis would be too slow. Automated defect-detection technology would enable inspectors to check bridges more frequently and perhaps identify problems before failures occur.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>File this under: You knew this was going to be an issue. <a href=\"https:\/\/www.axios.com\/border-robot-dog-hispanic-lawmakers-concern-14360a33-5d81-4cb3-9118-133a4bf67df9.html\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Axios this week<\/a> reported on a letter from a trio U.S. Congressional Democrats asking for a meeting with U.S. Customs and Border Protection about those Ghost Robotics dogs currently being piloted on the border. Among the notes is an issue with the phrase \u201crobotic dogs,\u201d which I just used in the previous sentence.<\/p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2279126\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption alignnone\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2279126\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout size-full wp-image-2279126\" id=\"caption-attachment-2279126\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:<\/strong> Ghost Robotics<\/p><\/div><\/p>\n<p>&#8220;It downplays the threat the robots pose to migrants arriving at our southern border and the part they play in a long history of surveillance and privacy violations in our border communities,\u201d the letter reads, in part.<\/p>\n<p>All right, more next week! In the meantime, please <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/newsletters\">subscribe<\/a>.<\/p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2191371\" style=\"width: 3210px\" class=\"wp-caption alignleft\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2191371\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout wp-image-2191371 size-full\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2021\/08\/actuator-blue.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"3200\" height=\"1800\" srcset=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2021\/08\/actuator-blue.jpg 3200w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2021\/08\/actuator-blue.jpg?resize=150,84 150w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2021\/08\/actuator-blue.jpg?resize=300,169 300w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2021\/08\/actuator-blue.jpg?resize=768,432 768w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2021\/08\/actuator-blue.jpg?resize=680,383 680w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2021\/08\/actuator-blue.jpg?resize=1536,864 1536w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2021\/08\/actuator-blue.jpg?resize=2048,1152 2048w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2021\/08\/actuator-blue.jpg?resize=50,28 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 3200px) 100vw, 3200px\" \/><p id=\"caption-attachment-2191371\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:<\/strong> Bryce Durbin\/TechCrunch<\/p><\/div><\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>I\u2019m writing this fresh off hosting TechCrunch Live this week with Jonathan Hurst of Agility Robotics and Bruce Leak of Playground Global. I\u2019ll be posting more about the session later this week, but in the meantime, it\u2019s got me thinking about carts, horses and the inherent ordering system therein. talks its first phone, ahead of a Q4 release"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\"><span class=\"featured__span-first-words\">Mobile World Congress<\/span> was going to be OSOM\u2019s big debut. After a few months of teases, the North American (U.S.\/Canadian) smartphone maker <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/12\/21\/after-essentials-failure-osom-plans-a-new-privacy-first-handset\/\">formed in the wake of Essential&#8217;s collapse<\/a> was going to show off its first handset, amid a week of major mobile news. But plans change. People decide maybe traveling to a major conference on another continent is still a tricky proposition. And sometimes a major chipmaker knocks on your door, asking if you\u2019d maybe like to push your launch back a quarter.<\/p>\n<p>OSOM was understandably convinced that pushing things back to launch a new device with the latest Qualcomm chip would be a prudent business decision. That meant, among other things, moving the OV1\u2019s release from a broad Q3 to an equally broad Q4. Not the end of the world. People buy a lot of phones in Q4, after all. And let\u2019s be real, by the time the third quarter rolls around, last year\u2019s big Snapdragon announcement is starting to feel a bit long in the tooth.<\/p>\n<p>For obvious reasons, the company can\u2019t disclose what the next chip will be. Given OSOM\u2019s focus on launching a flagship-quality device, however, it seems a fairly safe bet that it will be a successor to the Snapdragon 8 Gen 1 &#8212; whether that\u2019s the equivalent to a Snapdragon 8 Gen 1+ (this is getting unwiedly) or a Snapdragon 8 Gen 2 depends on the timing of launch and Qualcomm\u2019s roadmap.<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2278549\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<p>\u201cThey love that we\u2019re local. We have a longstanding history with the team of working directly with them,\u201d founder and CEO Jason Keats tells TechCrunch. \u201cOne of our partners is big enough that Qualcomm was like, \u2018holy crap, you\u2019re working with them? We want to be more involved with what you\u2019re doing, as well.\u2019 They came back and said OSOM has the opportunity to do something new, exciting. To change how all of this works. And I think, part of it is we aren\u2019t a huge company, so if their yields aren\u2019t gigantic, they don\u2019t need to worry about shipping me five million chips in a month.\u201d<\/p>\n<p>The firm has some wiggle room for release date, courtesy of $20 million in funding thus far. It\u2019s also \u201ca couple of weeks\u201d away from announcing its Series A. In spite of the relative humbleness around initial sales, Keats believes the company\u2019s direct sales approach and availability in North America and Europe through additional channels like Amazon will help it grow quickly, with an expectation of top five\/10 in global shipments with a year &#8212; with the very important caveat that all of the BBK companies (Oppo\/OnePlus, Vivo, Realme, iQOO) are all clumped together.<\/p>\n<p><img loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout alignnone wp-image-2279136 size-full\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/OSOM_USB_OFF-1-e1646320304732.png\" alt=\"\" width=\"3000\" height=\"1777\" \/><\/p>\n<p>As OSOM noted at the outset, privacy is the thing here. While we\u2019re awaiting additional details, the startup has offered some insight into &#8212; of all things &#8212; the USB-C that ships in the box. It features a physical switch that lets users toggle between simply charging and sharing data. It\u2019s a clever addition, and surprisingly, not one I\u2019ve seen before. Along with the switch is a blinking light that lets you know which mode it&#8217;s in. The cable will also be sold a standalone, pricing TBD.<\/p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n<p>\u201cWe\u2019re really so focused on giving users control and making sure they have privacy at their fingertips that we wanted to embed that in every single thing we build,\u201d the company\u2019s VP of Design, Dave Evans, tells TechCrunch. \u201cNot on the phone, but also everything you\u2019ve got in the box. Having that cable, where you can let people know at any time that they\u2019re just going to get power on the device or start transferring information, they\u2019re totally in control of that.\u201d<\/p>\n<p>The company offered up a smattering of additional details. The frame is stainless steel, sandwiched by Gorilla Glass Invictus on the front and ceramic on the rear, in white, matte black and a third \u201cfun\u201d color. Basically premium materials, all around.<\/p>\n<p>Part of the Qualcomm deal is around camera stuff integrated into the unannounced (presumably) Snapdragon chip. The OV1 will sport dual rear-facing cameras at 48- and 12-megapixels, coupled with a 16-megapixel up front &#8212; all Sony sensors.<\/p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2279466\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption alignnone\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2279466\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"breakout size-full wp-image-2279466\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/Spec-reveal-1-1.jpeg\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"683\" srcset=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/Spec-reveal-1-1.jpeg 1440w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/Spec-reveal-1-1.jpeg?resize=150,100 150w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/Spec-reveal-1-1.jpeg?resize=300,200 300w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/Spec-reveal-1-1.jpeg?resize=768,512 768w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/Spec-reveal-1-1.jpeg?resize=680,453 680w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/03\/Spec-reveal-1-1.jpeg?resize=50,33 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" \/><p id=\"caption-attachment-2279466\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:<\/strong> OSOM<\/p><\/div><\/p>\n<p>\u201cOne of the biggest mistakes Essential made was not focusing nearly as much as it should have on the camera,\u201d Keats says of his former employer. \u201cYou go to read reviews like, \u2018amazing design and engineering, kind of poor connectivity and awful camera experience.\u2019\u201d<\/p>\n<p>The device will sport dual physical SIM slots &#8212; the company notes that it doesn&#8217;t want its released tied to any carriers, following the Essential\/Sprint deal. The handset is also physically larger than the PH-1, due, in part to a larger battery, which is current rates at &#8220;beyond all-day.&#8221; The OV1 will also support Ultra-wideband.<\/p>\n<p>The precise date of launch is still TBD &#8212; as is price, though Keats told us earlier that the privacy-focused device will be \u201cwell under $1,000,\u201d which frankly qualifies as cheap for flagships these days. The news follows our own recent reporting that<a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/02\/nothing-smartphone-carl-pei\/\"> Nothing is getting into the smartphone game<\/a>, with a major announcement before next month. The firm notably purchased assets from following Essential&#8217;s implosion, though founder Carl Pei confirmed with TechCrunch that the move was for branding alone, as the firm briefly considered reviving its name.<\/p>\n<p>The handsets will arrive during a transitional period for an industry that has suffered from stagnant sales following a decade of explosive growth. The world of smartphones is, at very least, a mature one. Buying has slowed and people are generally satisfied with device quality that has improved dramatically in recent years. True disruption will have to happen bottom, up, as some of the market is currently controlled by a handful of players.<\/p>\n<p>OSOM, for its part, is betting big that privacy is enough of a differentiator to help it capture sales out of the get &#8212; well, privacy coupled with a decent marketing budget to drive some of those concerns home. The company also envisions itself as more than just a hardware maker &#8212; which, let&#8217;s be honest, you pretty much have to, these days.<\/p>\n<p>&#8220;We\u2019re building an entire solution to privacy,&#8221; says Keats. &#8220;We want to build devices and software and services that facilitate individual user privacy. And that could be as simple as integrating our software partners into our hardware to give them a real base for them to work, rather than just downloading their app from an app store. We can install it on a system level.&#8221;<\/p>\n<p>That includes partnering with companies on various solutions. In January, OSOM <a href=\"https:\/\/twitter.com\/OsomPrivacy\/status\/1480960357615214594\">officially announced<\/a> a &#8220;long-term strategic partnership&#8221; with HP, though specifics of the deal are still forthcoming.<\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>Mobile World Congress was going to be OSOM\u2019s big debut. After a few months of teases, the North American (U.S.\/Canadian) smartphone maker formed in the wake of Essential&#8217;s collapse was going to show off its first handset, amid a week of major mobile news. But plans change. Games just bought an entire Bandcamp, and it\u2019s not even Friday"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Happy Bandcamp Wednesday. Fortnite-maker Epic Games is <a href=\"https:\/\/blog.bandcamp.com\/2022\/03\/02\/bandcamp-is-joining-epic\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">treating itself to an entire Bandcamp<\/a>. The music download site announced the acquisition in a blog post today, adding that it will continue to function as a standalone entity with co-founder and CEO Ethan Diamond at its helm.<\/p>\n<p>\u201cWe share a vision of building the most open, artist-friendly ecosystem in the world, and together we\u2019ll be able to create even more opportunities for artists to be compensated fairly for their work,\u201d Diamond wrote in the post.<\/p>\n<p>The relatively business-as-usual approach includes the continued operation of Bandcamp\u2019s marketplace, community and editorial product, The Daily, as standalone entities. The company says it will also continue to operate Bandcamp Fridays as planned. The monthly event, which removes the service\u2019s cut from purchases, has been a wildly popular features as many musician have struggled to make ends meet amid touring shutdowns.<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2278961\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"twitter-tweet\" data-width=\"550\" data-dnt=\"true\">\n<p lang=\"en\" dir=\"ltr\">Bandcamp is joining Epic Games! Read more here: <a href=\"https:\/\/t.co\/dvLEhURZYG\">https:\/\/t.co\/dvLEhURZYG<\/a> <a href=\"https:\/\/t.co\/4t1MRvugmG\">pic.twitter.com\/4t1MRvugmG<\/a><\/p>\n<p>&mdash; bandcamp (@Bandcamp) <a href=\"https:\/\/twitter.com\/Bandcamp\/status\/1499068917947510788?ref_src=twsrc%5Etfw\">March 2, 2022<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n<p><script async src=\"https:\/\/platform.twitter.com\/widgets.js\" charset=\"utf-8\"><\/script><\/div>\n<p>\u201cSince our founding in 2008, we\u2019ve been motivated by the pursuit of our mission, which is to help spread the healing power of music by building a community where artists thrive through the direct support of their fans, the CEO adds. \u201cThat simple idea has worked well, with payments to artists and labels closing in on $1 billion USD.\u201d<\/p>\n<p>It\u2019s a strange acquisition from a thematic perspective, for sure. But Epic has been on something of an acquisition tear in recent years, with a ton of funding in its coffers, thanks largely to Fortnite\u2019s wild popularity. The pandemic, meanwhile, has forced many musicians to reassess their relationship with their work and the platforms they use to put it out into the world. Bandcamp has positioned itself as a far more musician-friendly service versus behemoths like Spotify, adding things like streaming concerts to its list of offerings.<\/p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n<p>\u201cWe couldn\u2019t be more excited to welcome the Bandcamp team to Epic Games,\u201d Epic VP Steve Allison said in <a href=\"https:\/\/www.epicgames.com\/site\/en-US\/news\/bandcamp-joining-epic-games-to-support-fair-open-platforms-for-artists-and-fans\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">a separate post<\/a>. \u201cBandcamp has built an incredible community and business where up and coming artists can succeed thanks to the direct support of their fans, with one of the best revenue models and terms in music. This aligns closely with Epic\u2019s approach to supporting creators across all media and enabling them to connect directly with their fans.\u201d<\/p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"fisALgaa9G\"><p><a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/03\/three-views-on-the-epic-bandcamp-deal\/\">3 views on the Epic-Bandcamp deal<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n<p><iframe class=\"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"&#8220;3 views on the Epic-Bandcamp deal&#8221; &#8212; TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/03\/three-views-on-the-epic-bandcamp-deal\/embed\/#?secret=2iuyMCAZL3#?secret=fisALgaa9G\" data-secret=\"fisALgaa9G\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>Happy Bandcamp Wednesday. Games","slug":"epic-games","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/348123"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=348123"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=348123"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=348123"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=348123"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}}],[],[],[],[]]}},{"id":2278702,"date":"2022-03-02T06:52:26","date_gmt":"2022-03-02T14:52:26","guid":{"rendered":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/?p=2278702"},"modified":"2022-03-02T10:01:38","modified_gmt":"2022-03-02T18:01:38","slug":"nothing-smartphone-carl-pei","status":"publish","type":"post","link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/02\/nothing-smartphone-carl-pei\/","title":{"rendered":"Carl Pei&#8217;s Nothing is working on a smartphone"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Seven years after co-founding OnePlus, Carl Pei left the smartphone maker in 2020 to launch his own venture. The company that became Nothing has, to date, launched a single product: <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/07\/29\/nothing-ear-1-review\/\">the Ear (1) transparent earbuds<\/a>. But it seems the executive is readying a return to the industry where he made his name.<\/p>\n<p>Nothing has been working on a smartphone for over a year and plans to announce it by next month, a source with direct knowledge of the matter told TechCrunch. The entrepreneur has shown off a prototype of the device in meetings with several key executives from the industry at this week\u2019s Mobile World Congress event in Barcelona, according to a source close to the company. TechCrunch has viewed a photo of one such meeting Pei had with Qualcomm&#8217;s chief executive.<\/p>\n<p>Details around the forthcoming device are thin, though the source notes that the product will share a similar design language and \u201celements of transparency\u201d seen in Nothing\u2019s first product. Released in 2021, the earbuds have shipped 400,000 units as of late-January, according to the London-based hardware startup.<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2278702\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<p>In an interview with TechCrunch last July, Pei noted that the company had several different devices on its roadmap, prior to teasing out the earbuds. \u201cWe have a lot of products in the pipeline. Earlier this year, we did a community crowdfunding round where we allocated $1.5 million to our community. That got bought up really quickly. But as part of that funding round, we had a deck with some of the products in development. Our products are code-named as Pok\u00e9mon, so there are a lot of Pok\u00e9mon on that slide. We have multiple categories that we\u2019re looking at, but we haven\u2019t really announced what those are.\u201d<\/p>\n<p>Pei certainly has the pedigree, as a co-founder and driving force behind OnePlus. The company&#8217;s first device arrived to an already mature &#8212; and seemingly saturated &#8212; category in 2014. Even so, it managed the difficult feat of breaking into the U.S. market on the strength of flagship build quality and mid-tier pricing. In 2021, OnePlus merged its hardware team with Oppo, its majority owner. Oppo, in turn, is owned by BBK, which is also behind key brands like Vivo, Realme and iQOO.<\/p>\n<p>The meetings come during a particularly staid edition of MWC. The industry has suffered from years of stagnating and declining sales, which have only been exacerbated by pandemic buying habits and subsequent supply chain slowdowns and chip shortages. The smartphone market is well-positioned for disruption, but doing so will be Herculean task, as handsets have grown from novelty to ubiquity and good quality products can be had for cheap. Nothing certainly has sufficient funding to make a splash, however. The firm has raised more than $70 million from scores of high-profile investors, including GV, Tony Fadell, Casey Neistat, Kevin Lin and Steve Huffman.<\/p>\n<p>It has also managed to build a following of its own by taking a similar community-driven focus, as well as a release schedule and marketing campaigns clearly informed by Pei&#8217;s interest in the sneaker market. But while the company was able to drive interest in a $99 pair of wireless earbuds, doing so in the well-established smartphone world is a different matter entirely.<\/p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n<p>Long-time leaders like LG and HTC have fallen away in recent years, while even big names like Google have found the industry to be an immense challenge. Players like Apple, Samsung and Xiaomi currently dominate global sales, followed by a number of the aforementioned brands under the BBK umbrella. Last week, newcomer Onward Mobility <a href=\"https:\/\/www.onwardmobility.com\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">announced<\/a> that it was scrapping plans to reboot the BlackBerry brand.<\/p>\n<div class=\"embed breakout\">\n<blockquote class=\"wp-embedded-content\" data-secret=\"O8iYZ8r2Dw\"><p><a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/07\/06\/nothing-founder-carl-pei-on-ear-1-and-building-a-hardware-startup-from-scratch\/\">Nothing founder Carl Pei on Ear (1) and building a hardware startup from scratch<\/a><\/p><\/blockquote>\n<p><iframe class=\"wp-embedded-content\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts\" security=\"restricted\" style=\"position: absolute; clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);\" title=\"&#8220;Nothing founder Carl Pei on Ear (1) and building a hardware startup from scratch&#8221; &#8212; TechCrunch\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/07\/06\/nothing-founder-carl-pei-on-ear-1-and-building-a-hardware-startup-from-scratch\/embed\/#?secret=7aCuklwAvR#?secret=O8iYZ8r2Dw\" data-secret=\"O8iYZ8r2Dw\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" frameborder=\"0\" marginwidth=\"0\" marginheight=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"><\/iframe><\/div>\n<p>Essential, famously, was unable to make a go of things, in spite of massive<a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2017\/08\/09\/essential-confirms-300m-funding-amazon-and-best-buy-retail-availability\/\"> $300 million in funding<\/a>, though one can point to plenty of missteps and other issues with the Andy Rubin-founded company. Pei&#8217;s firm subsequently purchased the Essential branding, a move he later told TechCrunch came when the company was considering adopting the name of the ill-fated hardware maker. A number of Essential employees, meanwhile, have gone on to launch their own new smartphone company, OSOM, which <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/12\/21\/after-essentials-failure-osom-plans-a-new-privacy-first-handset\/\">plans to release a device<\/a> this year.<\/p>\n<p>Nothing declined to comment on this report.<\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>Seven years after co-founding OnePlus, Carl Pei left the smartphone maker in 2020 to launch his own venture. The company that became Nothing has, to date, launched a single product: the Ear (1) transparent earbuds. But it seems the executive is readying a return to the industry where he made his name. Ionic smartwatches recalled following battery burn reports"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Fitbit this morning announced that it <a href=\"https:\/\/www.cpsc.gov\/Recalls\/2022\/Fitbit-Recalls-Ionic-Smartwatches-Due%20to-Burn-Hazard-One-Million-Sold-in-the-U-S\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">is participating<\/a> in a U.S. Consumer Product Safety Commission recall of its <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2017\/08\/28\/fitbit-is-betting-big-on-the-ionic-smartwatch\/\">Ionic smartwatch<\/a>\u00a0following reports of an overheating battery and potential burn injury.<\/p>\n<p>The CPSC adds in a report today that at least 115 people in the U.S. &#8212; and 59 internationally &#8212; have reported an overheating battery on the device. Those figures include a total of 118 reports of burns, with two reports of third-degree burns and four reports of second-degree burns.<\/p>\n<p>The CPSC notes, \u201cConsumers should immediately stop using the recalled Ionic smartwatches and contact Fitbit to receive pre-paid packaging to return the device.\u201d<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2278761\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<p>The recall applies to Ionic devices with the model number FB503, amounting to one million in the U.S. and 693,000 internationally. Fitbit is refunding the product to the tune of $299 (plus a discount on future devices, if you want to replace it).<\/p>\n<p>The Google-owned wearable maker says it participated in the recall &#8220;out of an abundance of caution.&#8221; The full Fitbit statement is as follows:<\/p>\n<blockquote><p>Customer safety is always Fitbit\u2019s top priority and out of an abundance of caution, we are conducting a voluntary recall of Fitbit Ionic smartwatches. We received a very limited number of injury reports &#8211; the totals in the CPSC announcement represent less than 0.01% of units sold &#8211; of the battery in Fitbit Ionic smartwatches overheating, posing a burn hazard. These incidents are very rare and this voluntary recall does not impact other Fitbit smartwatches or trackers.<\/p><\/blockquote>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>Fitbit this morning announced that it is participating in a U.S. Consumer Product Safety Commission recall of its Ionic smartwatch\u00a0following reports of an overheating battery and potential burn injury. halts product sales in Russia following Ukraine invasion"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Apple today confirmed that it has halted product sales in Russia following last week\u2019s invasion of neighboring Ukraine. The news comes days after the hardware giant pulled Sputnik and RT News from the App Store and <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/03\/01\/apple-pulls-russian-state-owned-media-outlets-rt-and-sputnik-from-global-app-stores\/\">disabled some Apple Pay services in the country<\/a>. Today&#8217;s news also finds its disabling certain Apple Maps features, following <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/02\/28\/google-disables-maps-live-traffic-tools-in-ukraine\/\">a similar move by Google<\/a>.<\/p>\n<p>A spokesperson for the company offered TechCrunch the following statement:<\/p>\n<blockquote><p>We are deeply concerned about the Russian invasion of Ukraine and stand with all of the people who are suffering as a result of the violence. We are supporting humanitarian efforts, providing aid for the unfolding refugee crisis, and doing all we can to support our teams in the region.<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2278363\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<p>We have taken a number of actions in response to the invasion. We have paused all product sales in Russia. Last week, we stopped all exports into our sales channel in the country. Apple Pay and other services have been limited. RT News and Sputnik News are no longer available for download from the App Store outside Russia. And we have disabled both traffic and live incidents in Apple Maps in Ukraine as a safety and precautionary measure for Ukrainian citizens.<\/p>\n<p>We will continue to evaluate the situation and are in communication with relevant governments on the actions we are taking. We join all those around the world who are calling for peace.<\/p><\/blockquote>\n<p>Last week, Ukrainian Vice Prime Minister Mykhailo Fedorov <a href=\"https:\/\/twitter.com\/FedorovMykhailo\/status\/1497327555690610689\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">penned an open letter<\/a> to CEO Tim Cook. \u201cI appeal to you and I am sure that you will not only hear, but also do everything possible to protect Ukraine, Europe and, finally, the entire democratic world from bloody authoritarian aggression \u2013 to stop supplying Apple services and products to the Russian Federation, including blocking access to [the] App Store,\u201d wrote Fedorov, who also serves as the country\u2019s Minister of Digital Transformation.<\/p>\n<p>Today&#8217;s news represents the biggest step from one of the world&#8217;s largest companies and finds Apple issuing a verbal rebuke of Russia&#8217;s actions in recent weeks, along side <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/02\/24\/russia-ukraine\/\">other tech leaders<\/a>.<\/p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n<p>&nbsp;<\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>Apple today confirmed that it has halted product sales in Russia following last week\u2019s invasion of neighboring Ukraine. The news comes days after the hardware giant pulled Sputnik and RT News from the App Store and disabled some Apple Pay services in the country. Today&#8217;s news also finds its disabling certain Apple Maps features, following spinoff, OPT, ramps up production of its 3D-printed nasal swab"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">If you\u2019ve heard of <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/07\/14\/massachusetts-startup-opt-industries-is-perfecting-a-3d-printed-nasal-swab-for-covid-19-tests\/amp\/\">OPT Industries<\/a> (and odds are you haven\u2019t), it\u2019s due to the MIT spinoff\u2019s roll in printing a novel nasal swab. It\u2019s the kind of thing that might not have gotten much play during a normal year, but made the rounds courtesy of that whole global pandemic thing.<\/p>\n<p>InstaSwab got some good ink in the design world due to its complex geometrical build constructed from ultra-thin polymer fibers measuring less than the width of a human hair.<\/p>\n<p>OPT\u2019s 3D printing system was seven years in the making, but its timing was certainly fortuitous, with the creation of a swab it says is \u201cup to 20 times more effective in bacterial sample elution.\u201d The need for such devices predates COVID-19, of course, and will certainly live on once the pandemic is (knock on wood) in the rearview.<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2276617\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2276621\" style=\"width: 810px\" class=\"wp-caption alignnone\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2276621\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"size-full wp-image-2276621\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/02\/new-swab-v5_reduced.gif\" alt=\"\" width=\"800\" height=\"450\" \/><p id=\"caption-attachment-2276621\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:<\/strong> OPT Industries<\/p><\/div><\/p>\n<p>It was, no doubt, precisely the manner of press an early-stage startup dreams of when time comes to start raising some major funding. Today, the company is making headlines again courtesy of a healthy $15 million Series A. Northpond Ventures leads this round, joining existing investors Crosslink Capital and E14 Fund, which is tied to MIT.<\/p>\n<p>InstaSwap will remain an integral part of the company\u2019s offering for now, with the firm planning to ramp up production on the cotton swab alternative. Along with the funding, OPT confirmed that LumiraDx has added it to its list of approved swabs &#8212; the first 3D-printed product to earn that distinction.<\/p>\n<p>\u201cAs an advanced manufacturing company, we see the value in building micro-scale technologies that solve macro-scale challenges,\u201d says founder and CEO Jifei Ou. \u201cOPT works with customers to design and manufacture a novel range of metamaterials and products for the healthcare, automotive, cosmetics, consumer goods industries and beyond. We will use the new financing to address the demand for InstaSwab, fuel product development, scale operations and grow the team.\u201d<\/p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2276622\" style=\"width: 610px\" class=\"wp-caption alignnone\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2276622\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"size-full wp-image-2276622\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/02\/Clear-Swab-Absorption-Elution_4-1.gif\" alt=\"\" width=\"600\" height=\"400\" \/><p id=\"caption-attachment-2276622\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:<\/strong> OPT Industries<\/p><\/div><\/p>\n<p>From that standpoint, the swab is certainly more than proof of concept here. But it\u2019s also a kind of launching pad for the underlying technology that powers OPT\u2019s additive manufacturing offering. RAMP 3D prints edge-to-edge rolls, allowing the system to produce high-resolution prints at a much faster scale than traditional methods. With the ability to print around the clock, the firm also believes it can play a pivotal role in addressing supply chain concerns &#8212; a long-time goal for additive manufacturing. Though it\u2019s traditionally had issues at scale.<\/p>\n<p>Toward the end of 2021, OPT moved into a 14,000-square-foot manufacturing facility in Medford, Massachusetts, near its initial headquarters of Bedford, in an effort to meet growing InstaSwab demand.<\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>If you\u2019ve heard of OPT Industries (and odds are you haven\u2019t), it\u2019s due to the MIT spinoff\u2019s roll in printing a novel nasal swab. It\u2019s the kind of thing that might not have gotten much play during a normal year, but made the rounds courtesy of that whole global pandemic thing. Wing drones hit 200,000 deliveries as it announces supermarket partnership"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Alphabet\u2019s drone service Wing this morning announced another milestone, as it hit 200,000 commercial deliveries. The number, which the firm says excludes test flights, comes half-a-year <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2021\/08\/25\/wing-approaches-100000-drone-deliveries-two-years-after-logan-australia-launch\/\">after it hit 100,000<\/a>. Australia, which has been the primary market for testing and commercial deployment, comprises 30,000 of those deliveries in the first two months of this year.<\/p>\n<p>Broken down further, Wing says it was up to more than 1,000 deliveries in a day, or one delivery every 25 seconds or so. The big round number arrives as it announces a commercial partnership with Coles, one of Australia\u2019s leading supermarket chains. The deal finds Wing delivering 250 different items, from food stuffs to healthcare products and toiletries, in Australia\u2019s capital, Canberra.<\/p>\n<p>Other recent additions to the delivery service include KFC and Vietnamese food from Roll\u2019d, as well as rapid COVID-19 tests from Friendly Grocer and first aid kids from St. John Ambulance QLD. Big, round number aside, the efficacy of drone delivery in urban areas is still a question mark, as many services are more actively eyeing terrestrial robotics as the future of last-mile delivery.<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2277778\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<p>The technology makes some sense for rural and other harder to reach locales, but Wing insists that its approach is right for city life, as well.<\/p>\n<p>\u201cIntegrating drone delivery into daily life isn\u2019t just an added convenience,\u201d Google says in a blog post today. \u201cIt holds the promise to reduce traffic congestion, accidents, and greenhouse gas emissions while growing sales for businesses all the while giving people more time back in their busy lives. If you want a peek into that future, just look to Australia.\u201d<\/p>\n<p>Amazon\u2019s competing service, Prime Air, meanwhile, has suffered layoffs during the pandemic, leaving questions around the viability of that delivery method.<\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>Alphabet\u2019s drone service Wing this morning announced another milestone, as it hit 200,000 commercial deliveries. Australia, which has been the primary market for testing and commercial deployment, comprises 30,000 of those deliveries in the first two months of this year. gets into the e-reader game with a combo note-taking device"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Nearly 20 years after the release of the first e-reader, the category isn\u2019t exactly a hotbed of activity. Amazon has ruled the roost for over a decade now, in spite of the best efforts of companies like Kobo (Rakuten) and what remains of Barnes &amp; Noble\u2019s hardware efforts.<\/p>\n<p>This week at MWC, embattled hardware maker Huawei announced that it\u2019s getting in on the action with the<a href=\"https:\/\/consumer.huawei.com\/en\/tablets\/matepad-paper\/specs\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"> MatePad Paper.<\/a> With a 10.3-inch E Ink display and the ability to take notes with the company\u2019s M-Pencil writing tool, offerings from reMarkable could ultimately be the best comparison point.<\/p>\n<p>Reviewing <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2020\/08\/27\/the-remarkable-2-improves-on-the-original-in-every-way-but-remains-firmly-in-its-niche\/\">that company\u2019s latest product<\/a>, Devin noted that the product \u201cremains firmly in its niche\u201d &#8212; which has been a bit of a theme with e ink products outside the Kindle universe. Huawei\u2019s hoping to push through with a device aimed at being a little bit of everything. At its heart, it\u2019s a massively sized reader with access to the 2 million or so titles in the Huawei Books store, as well as various other file types, including PDF.<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2277576\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<p>The device runs Huawei\u2019s proprietary HarmonyOS &#8212; a theme you\u2019re going to see across its MWC announcements, after having lost access to Android. But that effectively makes it something more akin to an e ink tablet, albeit Huawei\u2019s still limited access to third-party apps at the moment. What it does offer, however, are widgets for things like e-mail, notes and events.<\/p>\n<p>The large screen can be split to have something like reading open in one window and 26 ms writing with the M-Pencil in the other. One of the other upshots of the software is the ability to quickly move files back and forth between a MateBook laptop. There&#8217;s also built-in translation available.<\/p>\n<p>Another bit backing up the tablet thesis here is the inclusion of a microphone for notetaking and a pair of built-in speakers for audio books. There\u2019s a fingerprint reader for security and fast charging that allows for six days of reading with a 90-minute charge for the 3625 mAh battery.<\/p>\n<p>The downside to all of those features is the fact that the thing runs closer to a high-end tablet than e-reader, at \u20ac499. Due to ongoing geopolitical issues, it\u2019s hard to imagine this thing getting a proper U.S. release at any point. Still, it&#8217;s easily one of the most interesting consumer announcements at MWC this week &#8212; which may say more about the show than the product itself.<\/p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n<p><a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/mwc-2022\/\"><img src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/02\/mwc-2022-banner.jpg\" alt=\"Read more about MWC 2022 on TechCrunch\" \/><\/a><\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>Nearly 20 years after the release of the first e-reader, the category isn\u2019t exactly a hotbed of activity. Amazon has ruled the roost for over a decade now, in spite of the best efforts of companies like Kobo (Rakuten) and what remains of Barnes &amp; Noble\u2019s hardware efforts. This week at MWC, embattled hardware maker disables Maps live traffic tools in Ukraine"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">Alphabet today confirmed with TechCrunch that it has disabled certain Google Maps live traffic tools in Ukraine. The news, <a href=\"https:\/\/www.reuters.com\/technology\/google-temporarily-disables-google-maps-live-traffic-data-ukraine-2022-02-28\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">reported by Reuters<\/a>, comes after the company has consulted with local authorities as the country faces attacks from Russian forces. The features will remain on for local turn-by-turn navigation for drivers, while broader global access is disabled.<\/p>\n<p>The popular mapping technology has served a surprise role in the invasion of Ukraine since the outset. One research facility in California was able to spot the Russian approach toward the Ukrainian border hours before the \u201cspecial military operation\u201d was officially declared, using a combination of Maps traffic data and satellite imaging to spot traffic pileups.<\/p>\n<p>\u201cIn the old days, we would have relied on a reporter to show us what was happening on the ground,\u201d researcher Jeffrey Lewis <a href=\"https:\/\/www.washingtonpost.com\/technology\/2022\/02\/25\/google-maps-ukraine-invasion\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">told The Washington Post.<\/a> \u201cAnd today, you can open Google Maps and see people fleeing Kyiv.\u201d<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2277449\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<p>Alphabet\u2019s move appears to stem from concerns that the information could similarly be exploited by the Russian military to track Ukrainian troop movement. The corporation hasn\u2019t offered anything in the way of specifics on the matter, including when the switch was flipped or whether it\u2019s taken similar action amid other global conflicts.<\/p>\n<p>On the ground, Ukrainians have been taking a far more old-school approach to the matter. The government\u2019s road agency, Ukravtodor, <a href=\"https:\/\/www.washingtonpost.com\/world\/2022\/02\/26\/ukraine-russia-roads-signs-facebook\/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">began removing<\/a> road signs in an attempt to confuse Russian forces.<\/p>\n<p>\u201cLet\u2019s help them get straight to hell,\u201d it wrote on social media on Saturday. \u201cUkravtodor calls on all road organizations, territorial communities, local governments to immediately begin dismantling nearby road signs.\u201d<\/p>\n<p>Google <a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/02\/24\/russia-ukraine\/\">didn&#8217;t offer comment<\/a> directly on the invasion last week when asked by TechCrunch.<\/p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>Alphabet today confirmed with TechCrunch that it has disabled certain Google Maps live traffic tools in Ukraine. The news, reported by Reuters, comes after the company has consulted with local authorities as the country faces attacks from Russian forces. The features will remain on for local turn-by-turn navigation for drivers, while broader global access is new ThinkPad kicks off Qualcomm\u2019s new Snapdragon laptop platform"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\">After dominating the world of high-end mobile processors for so long, Qualcomm\u2019s got a laptop it would like to sell you. Announced at the tail end of last year as part of the annual Snapdragon summit, the Snapdragon 8cx Gen 3 marks the chipmaker\u2019s latest foray into the world of laptop components.<\/p>\n<p>As the name suggests, this marks Qualcomm\u2019s third major foray into the category. It arrives at time when Apple in-house chip production has given way to its own ARM-based laptop chips &#8212; to some pretty spectacular performance results. Qualcomm is hoping to do the same, leveraging mobile performance games into a Windows 11 Pro-based laptop.<\/p>\n<p><div id=\"attachment_2276553\" style=\"width: 1034px\" class=\"wp-caption alignnone\"><img aria-describedby=\"caption-attachment-2276553\" loading=\"lazy\" class=\"wp-image-2276553 size-full\" src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/02\/Screen-Shot-2022-02-24-at-3.45.48-PM.jpg\" alt=\"\" width=\"1024\" height=\"643\" srcset=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/02\/Screen-Shot-2022-02-24-at-3.45.48-PM.jpg 2082w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/02\/Screen-Shot-2022-02-24-at-3.45.48-PM.jpg?resize=150,94 150w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/02\/Screen-Shot-2022-02-24-at-3.45.48-PM.jpg?resize=300,188 300w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/02\/Screen-Shot-2022-02-24-at-3.45.48-PM.jpg?resize=768,482 768w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/02\/Screen-Shot-2022-02-24-at-3.45.48-PM.jpg?resize=680,427 680w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/02\/Screen-Shot-2022-02-24-at-3.45.48-PM.jpg?resize=1536,965 1536w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/02\/Screen-Shot-2022-02-24-at-3.45.48-PM.jpg?resize=2048,1287 2048w, https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/02\/Screen-Shot-2022-02-24-at-3.45.48-PM.jpg?resize=50,31 50w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1024px) 100vw, 1024px\" \/><p id=\"caption-attachment-2276553\" class=\"wp-caption-text\"><strong>Image Credits:<\/strong> Lenovo<\/p><\/div><\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2276547\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<p>As rumors swirl around a Microsoft Surface device built around the platform, Lenovo just used Mobile World Congress to launch its own device, the ThinkPad X13s. The advantages of the platform are apparent at first glance &#8212; adhering to the longstanding promises of shifting to ARM architecture in a laptop form factor. Namely, it\u2019s super thin and light, has built-in 5G, some stellar battery life and builds atop several generations of Qualcomm security advancements.<\/p>\n<p>Lenovo\u2019s an ideal first partner here. For one thing, the ThinkPad brand is synonymous with work laptops for many. Lenovo may also be the most eager laptop maker when it comes to exploring new avenues.<\/p>\n<p>Hopping over the spec sheet for a moment, the 13-inch laptop weighs in at 2.35 pounds &#8212; roughly half a pound lighter than the 2020 MacBook Air. At 0.53 inches thick, it sits comfortably within the Air\u2019s 0.16 to 0.63-inch range. Battery life is really the thing here &#8212; with a stated 28 hours of video playback. An exec in a briefing ahead of the event noted he\u2019d taken the system to New York for an overnight trip and never plugged it in &#8212; which seems wholly believable.<\/p>\n<p><span class=\"embed-youtube embed breakout embed--video\" style=\"text-align:center; display: block;\"><iframe class=\"youtube-player\" width=\"640\" height=\"360\" src=\"https:\/\/www.youtube.com\/embed\/FjRTpti2-o8?version=3&#038;rel=1&#038;showsearch=0&#038;showinfo=1&#038;iv_load_policy=1&#038;fs=1&#038;hl=en-US&#038;autohide=2&#038;wmode=transparent\" allowfullscreen=\"true\" style=\"border:0;\" sandbox=\"allow-scripts allow-same-origin allow-popups allow-presentation\"><\/iframe><\/span><\/p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n<p>Other Qualcomm benefits include the addition of a computer vision processor for improved log-in authentication. The system\u2019s body is made from 90% recycled magnesium, and yeah, the pointing stick remains in-tact.<\/p>\n<p>The system is set to arrive in May, priced at $1,099.<\/p>\n<p><a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/mwc-2022\/\"><img src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/02\/mwc-2022-banner.jpg\" alt=\"Read more about MWC 2022 on TechCrunch\" \/><\/a><\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>After dominating the world of high-end mobile processors for so long, Qualcomm\u2019s got a laptop it would like to sell you. Announced at the tail end of last year as part of the annual Snapdragon summit, the Snapdragon 8cx Gen 3 marks the chipmaker\u2019s latest foray into the world of laptop components. 2022","slug":"mwc-2022","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/576981147"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=576981147"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=576981147"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=576981147"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=576981147"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}},{"id":182541,"link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/qualcomm\/","name":"Qualcomm","slug":"qualcomm","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/182541"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=182541"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=182541"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=182541"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=182541"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}},{"id":383908,"link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/snapdragon\/","name":"snapdragon","slug":"snapdragon","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/383908"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=383908"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=383908"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=383908"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=383908"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}},{"id":1558,"link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/thinkpad\/","name":"Thinkpad","slug":"thinkpad","taxonomy":"post_tag","_links":{"self":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags\/1558"}],"collection":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tags"}],"about":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/taxonomies\/post_tag"}],"wp:post_type":[{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/posts?tags=1558"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/battlefield-companies?tags=1558"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc-media-gallery?tags=1558"},{"href":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-json\/wp\/v2\/tc_video?tags=1558"}],"curies":[{"name":"wp","href":"https:\/\/api.w.org\/{rel}","templated":true}]}}],[],[],[],[]]}},{"id":2276915,"date":"2022-02-25T10:48:33","date_gmt":"2022-02-25T18:48:33","guid":{"rendered":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/?p=2276915"},"modified":"2022-02-25T10:48:42","modified_gmt":"2022-02-25T18:48:42","slug":"will-mobile-world-congress-be-more-of-the-same","status":"publish","type":"post","link":"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/2022\/02\/25\/will-mobile-world-congress-be-more-of-the-same\/","title":{"rendered":"Will Mobile World Congress be more of the same?"},"content":{"rendered":"<p id=\"speakable-summary\"><span class=\"featured__span-first-words\">I\u2019m not sure<\/span> precisely when the change occurred, but at some point Mobile World Congress became the smartphone show. It\u2019s a fine thing to be in the world of tech trade shows &#8212; and certainly has a kind of outward-facing excitement that\u2019s largely lacking in the world of cellular infrastructure.<\/p>\n<p>Big booths and flashy press conferences from mobile giants are precisely the kind of news-generating content that bring the eyes of the world onto what might otherwise be a trade-only event. Hardware companies got locked into an announcement cycle tied to these shows. CES is where you get the home electronics, the wearables, the dishwashers and, eventually, the cars. But MWC is all about the phones.<\/p>\n<p>But the last several years have had a profoundly cooling effect on the smartphone. Beyond the inevitable shift from novelty to necessity, smartphone sales were already on a downward trajectory before the global pandemic. Buying habits slowed as users grew less attached to the carrier upgrade cycle. And as the price of premium handsets ballooned beyond $1,000, the pace of must-upgrade features slowed.<\/p><div id=\"tc-target-mid-article-2276915\" class=\"tc-target-mid-article ad-unit--size-auto\"><\/div>\n<p>As much as marketing departments might try to convince you otherwise, generational technology breakthroughs don\u2019t happen every year in consumer electronics. There\u2019s an ironic monkey\u2019s paw-style twist in all of this, too. As manufacturers rushed to outdo one another, smartphones largely got pretty good across the board. If you\u2019re willing to spend more than, say, $500 or $600, it\u2019s hard to go too wrong these days.<\/p>\n<p>Sure, some devices are better than others (I would likely be out of a job, otherwise), but industry advances have made products more durable, with longer-lasting batteries and better specs. As a result, planned obsolescence ain\u2019t what it used to be. There will certainly always be a small and enthusiastic contingent of fans who demand an annual upgrade, regardless of specifics (I probably work with a few of them). But on the whole, phones are better and people hold onto them for longer &#8212; a net positive for landfills, but a decided mark on sales.<\/p>\n<p>These things are to be expected from a mature category. The iPhone turned 15 year this year. The first Android device hits that milestone next year. But downward-trending sales figures were accelerated by the pandemic. First there was the simple fact that people weren\u2019t leaving their homes. Disposable income became a motivating factor as some lost jobs and others went on hiatus (to say nothing of the ensuing Great Resignation). What money people were spending on electronics was going toward outfitting home offices.<\/p>\n<p>Then came the supply chain stoppages and chip shortages. Meaning those people who wanted to make an upgrade couldn\u2019t in many markets. And unsurprisingly, these issues have had a disproportionate impact on smaller companies with far less leverage against chip and component manufacturers.<\/p><div class=\"piano-inline-promo\"><\/div>\n<p>It was destined to be a weird MWC by any measure. In 2020, it became one of the first major tech events to pull the plug, a month and a half after CES got by just under the wire. Last year\u2019s show happened at a much smaller scale. This year, the CES\/MWC fortunes flipped a bit, with the latter seemingly having missed the worst of the omicron variant, which gave some of the biggest names in tech cold feet ahead of the Vegas show. Not to mention the other major global event set to have an impact here.<\/p>\n<p>I won\u2019t be at the show this year. In the end, it really didn\u2019t make a lot of sense, as much as I continue to mourn missing out on a week in Barcelona. It\u2019s been one of the great rewards of this job. It\u2019s a fascinating show in one of the world\u2019s great cities that\u2019s been rife with all manner of weird TechCrunch adventures. Maybe I\u2019ll write a memoir some day for the eight people who might be interested in such a thing.<\/p>\n<p>Anecdotally, there doesn\u2019t seem to be much buzz for a major show set to start a few days from now. In addition to general weirdness around big, in-person events, there have been a confluence of factors that appear to point to the beginning of the end of MWC\u2019s days as the world\u2019s premier smartphone launchpad. There\u2019s certainly life left as a major event for mobile networks and infrastructure, even if some of the outward-facing luster has worn off.<\/p>\n<p>There\u2019s been a broader trend of companies going the Apple route and opting to launch devices at their own events on their own terms. This, again, has been accelerated by the pandemic, as companies were forced to put their own infrastructure in place for remote presentations. Samsung did precisely that earlier this month, with its S22 launch. Of course, not every company has the pull of an Apple or Samsung (or, for that matter, Google), so tying themselves to an event like MWC or CES still makes sense.<\/p>\n<p>The mobile industry in general has undergone some dramatic transformations over the past few years, as well. LG stopped making phones. HTC maybe still makes them, but at the very least has backed away from the category in dramatic fashion &#8212; notable for the maker of the aforementioned first Android phone. Huawei, meanwhile, is dealing with a lot these days, including sanctions that have precluded its use of the Android operating system and Qualcomm chips. Though perhaps we\u2019ll see some real-deal HarmonyOS handsets?<\/p>\n<p>On the latter front, I think it\u2019s fair to say that Qualcomm\u2019s Snapdragon release cycle has sucked some of the air out of the Fira de Barcelona. While the use of the latest Snapdragon flagship isn\u2019t really a differentiator (Qualcomm owns just under a third of the global mobile chip market), companies can gain a slight advantage by being one of the first to market with it. With Qualcomm\u2019s big event now happening each December, launches keep creeping up earlier and earlier in the year.<\/p>\n<p>Lenovo just announced a new Motorola handset, the Edge Plus, which finds the largely budget-focused brand brushing right up against the $1,000 mark. That means its parent will likely be sticking to laptops. Similarly, Samsung is expected to be using the show to announce a new Galaxy Book, having already showed the world the Galaxy S22. I suppose both technically qualify as \u201cmobile,\u201d but neither really bolster MWC\u2019s image as the smartphone show.<\/p>\n<p>That doesn\u2019t leave a lot of major players. In addition to some potential noise from a Huawei camp looking to claw its way out of the muck, other Chinese manufacturers could fill some of the vacuum here. Oppo\u2019s OnePlus brand unveiled its flagship around CES, but the parent company may well use this as an opportunity to get some press.<\/p>\n<p>Ditto for TCL, which continues working to establish its own brand name. Xiaomi and Vivo, meanwhile, are working to establish themselves outside of their native market and India &#8212; though the number one and two smartphone markets already present plenty of room for growth.<\/p>\n<p>With MWC happening next week, it\u2019s too early to definitively say where this all leaves the show &#8212; and the industry in general. At best, it\u2019s a transition period at a weird time for hardware makers &#8212; an awkward adolescence as the industry looks toward the horizon in hopes of hitting upon the next major disrupter.<\/p>\n<p><a href=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/tag\/mwc-2022\/\"><img src=\"https:\/\/techcrunch.com\/wp-content\/uploads\/2022\/02\/mwc-2022-banner.jpg\" alt=\"Read more about MWC 2022 on TechCrunch\" \/><\/a><\/p>\n","protected":false},"excerpt":{"rendered":"<p>I\u2019m not sure precisely when the change occurred, but at some point Mobile World Congress became the smartphone show. It\u2019s a fine thing to be in the world of tech trade shows &#8212; and certainly has a kind of outward-facing excitement that\u2019s largely lacking in the world of cellular infrastructure. Big booths and flashy press
